import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 3D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input and kernel, and optional stride.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple of ints): Size of the convolution kernel in the form (kernel_size_depth, kernel_size_height, kernel_size_width).
        stride (tuple of ints, optional): Stride of the convolution in the form (stride_depth, stride_height, stride_width). Defaults to (1, 1, 1).
        padding (tuple of ints, optional): Padding applied to the input in the form (padding_depth, padding_height, padding_width). Defaults to (0, 0, 0).
        output_padding (tuple of ints, optional): Additional size added to one side of the output shape. Defaults to (0, 0, 0).
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: tuple = (1, 1, 1), padding: tuple = (0, 0, 0), output_padding: tuple = (0, 0, 0), groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding, groups=groups, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 3D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 32
kernel_size = (3, 5, 7)
depth = 12
height = 24
width = 48
stride = (2, 2, 2)
padding = (1, 2, 3)
output_padding = (1, 1, 1)
groups = 4

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, groups]

But we can't be sure. However, writing a custom direct kernel that maps well to the GPU could beat PyTorch's overhead (since PyTorch has Python overhead and cuDNN heuristics). Alternatively, we could implement the transposed convolution via a regular convolution on an upsampled tensor using PyTorch's conv3d, but that would call cuDNN again. We need custom CUDA to try to beat it. Given the smallish output size (28M elements), a thread-per-output kernel with simple gather might be effective. But we need to think about how to make it as fast as possible: 1. Flatten output index and use a 1D grid of threads. 2. Each thread computes its output coordinates via division/mod or precomputation. 3. Loop over input channels and kernel positions, skipping invalid ones quickly. 4. Use vectorized loads if possible? Probably not necessary. 5. Keep weight in constant memory? Or rely on L1 cache. 6. Use shared memory for input or weight tiles? Could help. Given that groups=4, C_in_per_group=8, C_out_per_group=8, K_d=3, K_h=5, K_w=7. We can design a kernel where each block handles a tile of spatial positions and all output channels of a group. For instance, blockDim.x = 8 (output channels per group), blockDim.y = some W_tile, blockDim.z = some H_tile? Since total threads per block must be <= 1024. For example, blockDim = (8, 8, 8) = 512 threads. Block could compute: - 1 batch, 1 group, some D, some H, some W, all 8 output channels. - So blockIdx maps to (b, g, d_start, h_start, w_start). - Threads within block: threadIdx.x = out_c (0..7), threadIdx.y = d offset, threadIdx.z = something. But to maximize memory coalescing for output writes, the fastest varying dimension should be W (last dimension). Output layout is NCDHW. So for a fixed (b,c,d,h), consecutive w are contiguous in memory. Thus, threads in a warp should differ in w to get coalesced writes. However, if threadIdx.x = out_c, then threads in a warp (32 threads) will be 4 groups of 8 out_c? That is not contiguous in memory because channels are the second dimension; after W_out=96 elements, next is next channel. So memory stride between out_c difference is D_out*H_out*W_out = 24*48*96 = 110592 floats = 442KB. That's huge; non-coalesced. Better: have threadIdx.x map to the W dimension. Then threads in a warp write consecutive memory addresses (if they correspond to same b,c,d,h). But then each thread computes one output channel? We can have each warp compute a single output channel for a range of W. Since W_out=96, a warp of 32 threads covers 32 W values. Two warps cover 64, third covers remainder. That gives coalesced writes. Similarly, if we have a block with dimensions (32, 4, 1), where x=W, y=some other. But we also want to reuse input across output channels. If a warp computes one output channel, then different warps compute different output channels. Input is reused across warps via L2 cache (same spatial positions). Weight loads differ per warp. Alternatively, have each thread compute a small vector of W (e.g., 4 consecutive W) to amortize coordinate computation and increase arithmetic intensity. But perhaps the simplest is a 1D kernel where each thread computes one output element. We can flatten the output linear index. The linear index order is NCDHW (PyTorch default). So consecutive linear indices differ in W first, then H, then D, then C, then N. Thus, if we launch one thread per linear output element, threads in a block will be contiguous in W, then H, etc. This means writes are coalesced because consecutive threads write to consecutive memory addresses (as long as block size is multiple of warp size). That's perfect. So a 1D thread-per-output kernel with linear indexing is simple and gives coalesced writes. Reads from input will not be coalesced because different threads may read different input locations. But maybe it's okay. Now, we need to compute the output coordinates from the linear index. For a 1D kernel, each thread gets `idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x`. Then we compute: tmp = idx; x = tmp % W_out; tmp /= W_out; y = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; z = tmp % D_out; tmp /= D_out; c = tmp % out_channels; tmp /= out_channels; b = tmp; This requires division/mod. On modern GPUs, integer div/mod is somewhat expensive but okay if done once per thread. We can also precompute strides. Then group = c / out_channels_per_group; out_c = c % out_channels_per_group; Then accumulate over in_c and kernel positions. We need to handle output_padding. In the formula for transposed conv (cross-correlation), the mapping from output (z,y,x) to input (z',y',x') is: z_in = (z + padding_d - kd) / stride_d This is valid if (z + padding_d - kd) % stride_d == 0 and z_in in [0, D_in-1]. But let's double-check the formula. In conv3d: out[z] = sum_{kd} in[z * stride - padding + kd] * w[kd] (with bounds checks). Wait, standard conv cross-correlation with stride s and padding p: out[n,c_out,z] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kd} in[n, c_in, z*s - p + kd] * w[c_out, c_in, kd] (+bias). So the input coordinate accessed is z_in = z*s - p + kd. Thus, for a given output z_out and kernel offset kd, input accessed is z_in = z_out * stride - padding + kd. Transpose conv is the transpose of this linear operation. That means if we consider the input-output relationship, the weight for connecting input z_in to output z_out is w[kd] where z_in = z_out * stride - padding + kd. Rearranging: z_out = (z_in + padding - kd) / stride. So for a fixed input z_in and kernel kd, the output coordinate is z_out = (z_in + padding - kd) / stride, which must be integer. Let's derive from conv. Consider 1D conv: y[s] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w[k] * x[s*S - P + k] (assuming stride S, padding P). Valid when 0 <= s*S - P + k < L_in. Now transpose conv is the adjoint. If we have input x of length L_in and output y of length L_out, the transpose operation maps output gradient dy to input gradient dx: dx[t] = sum_{s,k: t = s*S - P + k} w[k] * dy[s]. For a fixed output position s and kernel k, it contributes to input position t = s*S - P + k. So for a fixed output s, the kernel scan over k and writes to t = s*S - P + k. Thus, the transpose convolution (forward) takes an input x (length L_in) and produces output y (length L_out) such that y[s] = sum_{t,k: t = s*S - P + k} x[t] * w[k]? Wait, no. The forward transpose conv is defined as the operation whose Jacobian transpose is the conv Jacobian. The forward of transpose conv is: y = conv_transpose(x) where y[s] = sum_{t,k: s*S - P + k = t} x[t] * w[k]? That seems wrong dimensionally. Let's be precise. Let conv operator C map input x (size L_in) to output y (size L_out): y[s] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w[k] * x[s*S - P + k] (with x indexed as zero outside bounds). Then the adjoint C^T maps z (size L_out) to v (size L_in): v[t] = sum_{s=0}^{L_out-1} sum_{k=0}^{K-1} w[k] * z[s] where t = s*S - P + k. Equivalently, v[t] = sum_{s,k: s*S - P + k = t} w[k] * z[s]. But will it be fast enough? Let's consider. CUDA kernel requirements: - Must handle arbitrary kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, groups. - Must be numerically equivalent. Let's design a generic kernel: ```cuda template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_forward_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int batch_size, int in_channels, int out_channels, int in_depth, int in_height, int in_width, int out_depth, int out_height, int out_width, int kernel_d, int kernel_h, int kernel_w, int stride_d, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_d, int pad_h, int pad_w, int groups) { // output linear index int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * out_channels * out_depth * out_height * out_width; if (idx >= total) return; // decompose idx int tmp = idx; int ow = tmp % out_width; tmp /= out_width; int oh = tmp % out_height; tmp /= out_height; int od = tmp % out_depth; tmp /= out_depth; int oc = tmp % out_channels; tmp /= out_channels; int n = tmp; int out_channels_per_group = out_channels / groups; int in_channels_per_group = in_channels / groups; int g = oc / out_channels_per_group; int o = oc % out_channels_per_group; scalar_t acc = 0; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_channels_per_group; ++ic) { int ic_global = g * in_channels_per_group + ic; for (int kd = 0; kd < kernel_d; ++kd) { int id_val = od + pad_d - kd; if (id_val % stride_d != 0) continue; int id = id_val / stride_d; if (id < 0 || id >= in_depth) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < kernel_h; ++kh) { int ih_val = oh + pad_h - kh; if (ih_val % stride_h != 0) continue; int ih = ih_val / stride_h; if (ih < 0 || ih >= in_height) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kernel_w; ++kw) { int iw_val = ow + pad_w - kw; if (iw_val % stride_w != 0) continue; int iw = iw_val / stride_w; if (iw < 0 || iw >= in_width) continue; scalar_t in_val = input[((((n * in_channels + ic_global) * in_depth + id) * in_height + ih) * in_width + iw)]; scalar_t w_val = weight[((((((g * out_channels_per_group + o) * in_channels_per_group + ic) * kernel_d + kd) * kernel_h + kh) * kernel_w + kw)]; acc += in_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) { acc += bias[oc]; } output[idx] = acc; } ``` But we need to be careful about weight layout. We plan to permute weight in Python to layout (groups, out_channels_per_group, in_channels_per_group, kD, kH, kW). Let's do that. Wait, the weight shape originally is (in_channels, out_channels_per_group, kD, kH, kW). We can do: ```python weight = self.weight.view(groups, in_channels_per_group, out_channels_per_group, kD, kH, kW) weight = weight.permute(0, 2, 1, 3, 4, 5).contiguous() ``` Then in CUDA, weight index is: `((g * out_channels_per_group + o) * in_channels_per_group + ic) * k_volume + kd * k_h * k_w + kh * k_w + kw`. This matches. Now, we need to think about performance. The innermost loops over kw and kh have conditionals. The compiler might not optimize this well. We could restructure by precomputing valid kd, kh, kw ranges or iterating only over valid ones. Observation: The condition `id_val % stride_d != 0` depends only on `od` and `kd`. So for a given `od`, the valid `kd`s are those with a specific parity (mod stride). If stride is small (<=3), we can create small arrays in registers to iterate only over valid kernel offsets. But for generic stride, we can precompute a lookup table per dimension. However, maybe we can do better: For each thread, precompute the set of valid kd, kh, kw. Since kernel sizes are small (max maybe 7), we can just loop and skip with if. Branch divergence is not too bad because within a warp, `od` may differ, leading to different valid kd. But if threads in a warp have different `od`, they will diverge on the kd loop. However, the loop over kd is only 3 iterations, so divergence cost is small. But the main issue is memory access pattern for input. Since input index depends on `kd`, `kh`, `kw`, and `ic`, threads in a warp will access scattered input locations. This may lead to many cache misses. Can we improve input locality? Possibly by reordering loops: iterate over input channels and spatial, but load a block of input into shared memory and compute partial sums. But given the complexity, maybe the naive kernel is okay. Let's consider an alternative: implement the transposed conv as a "scatter" (atomicAdd) from each input element to output. Input has ~3.5M elements. Each input element contributes to out_channels_per_group * K_volume output positions? Wait, for each input element, it contributes to all out channels in its group and all kernel positions. That is 8 * 105 = 840 output positions per input channel. For 32 input channels, each input spatial position (B, D_in, H_in, W_in) has 32*840 = 26,880 output contributions. With batch 8 and spatial 12*24*48=13824, total input elements = 8*32*13824 = 3,538,944. Total contributions = 3,538,944 * 105 * 8 = 2.97 billion. Same as before. If we scatter with atomicAdd, each thread processes one input element and loops over out channels and kernel positions, atomically adding to output. This requires writing to output coalesced? No, writes are scattered (atomic to random locations). That would be terrible for performance due to atomic contention. So gather is better. What about using shared memory for a tile of output? For example, a block computes a tile of output spatial positions and all output channels of a group. Then it loads the corresponding input tile (which is input positions that map to that output tile). Since each output tile depends on a relatively small input region (roughly output_tile_size / stride plus kernel), we can load that input into shared memory and reuse it across threads computing different output channels. But output is 108MB, input is 13.5MB. Shared memory is at most 48KB or 96KB per SM. A tile of input of size, say, 8x8x8x8 (channels*D*H*W) = 4096 floats = 16KB. We can load that and reuse it. But the mapping is complex. Maybe we can do an algorithm similar to direct convolution but using a "macro-kernel". Given the constraints of writing inline CUDA, maybe the naive gather kernel with some optimizations (like using `int64_t` for indexing? No, `int` is fine) and ensuring contiguous output writes is enough to beat cuDNN for this particular shape. Wait, but I should check the GPU specs. The system might be using an A100 or similar. Let me analyze the problem. I need to implement a 3D transposed convolution (ConvTranspose3d) as a custom CUDA kernel. First, let me understand the reference operation: - ConvTranspose3d with groups > 1 (groups=4) - Input shape: (batch_size=8, in_channels=32, depth=12, height=24, width=48) - Output channels: 32 - Kernel size: (3, 5, 7) - Stride: (2, 2, 2) - Padding: (1, 2, 3) - Output padding: (1, 1, 1) - Groups: 4 With groups=4: - in_channels per group = 32 / 4 = 8 - out_channels per group = 32 / 4 = 8 A transposed convolution can be viewed as a regular convolution with: 1. Dilating the input by inserting zeros between elements (based on stride) 2. Padding around the dilated input 3. Performing a regular convolution Or equivalently: it's equivalent to convolving the input with the weight tensor, but with the weight shape interpreted differently. Actually, a cleaner way to think about ConvTranspose3d is: - Output shape formula (for each dim): L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 With default dilation=1: - D_out = (12 - 1) * 2 - 2*1 + 1*(3-1) + 1 + 1 = 11*2 - 2 + 2 + 2 = 22 + 2 = 24 - H_out = (24 - 1) * 2 - 2*2 + 1*(5-1) + 1 + 1 = 23*2 - 4 + 4 + 2 = 46 + 2 = 48 - W_out = (48 - 1) * 2 - 2*3 + 1*(7-1) + 1 + 1 = 47*2 - 6 + 6 + 2 = 94 + 2 = 96 So output shape: (8, 32, 24, 48, 96) For group transposed convolution, it's essentially a transposed convolution applied per group. The mathematical operation for ConvTranspose3d with groups is essentially: For each group g: output[:, g*out_cpg:(g+1)*out_cpg, ...] = conv_transpose3d(input[:, g*in_cpg:(g+1)*in_cpg, ...], weight[g*in_cpg:(g+1)*in_cpg, ...]) But actually, conv_transpose3d weight is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). Great, now I can implement the kernel. For performance, let me think about the memory access patterns: - Input: [N, C_in, D_in, H_in, W_in] - Weight: [C_in, C_out/g, kD, kH, kW] - Output: [N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out] With our specific sizes: N=8, C_in=32, D_in=12, H_in=24, W_in=48 C_out=32, D_out=24, H_out=48, W_out=96 kD=3, kH=5, kW=7 g=4, in_cpg=8, out_cpg=8 stride=(2,2,2), padding=(1,2,3) Wait, I need to recalculate with the correct padding formula: pd=1, ph=2, pw=3. D_out = (12-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 1 = 22 - 2 + 3 + 1 = 24 ✓ H_out = (24-1)*2 - 2*2 + 5 + 1 = 46 - 4 + 5 + 1 = 48 ✓ W_out = (48-1)*2 - 2*3 + 7 + 1 = 94 - 6 + 7 + 1 = 96 ✓ So output size is (8, 32, 24, 48, 96). Now, for the kernel implementation, I need to consider what's most performant. Option 1: Output-stationary, each thread computes 1 output voxel for all output channels in its group. - Block: (out_cpg, tile_ow) = (8, 16) - Grid: (N, G, D_out, H_out, ceil(W_out/16)) - Each thread: loop over ic, kd, kh, kw and accumulate. Option 2: Input-stationary, iterate over input and scatter to output. - This might have better utilization since all kernel positions are valid (for input-stationary, every input position contributes to all kernel positions, no invalid checks needed). - But it requires atomic adds or careful synchronization if multiple input positions write to the same output. Actually, for transpose conv, the input-stationary approach is quite natural: For each input position (id, ih, iw) and each kernel position (kd, kh, kw): od = id*sd + kd - pd oh = ih*sh + kh - ph ow = iw*sw + kw - pw if all valid: output[b, oc, od, oh, ow] += input[b, ic, id, ih, iw] * weight[ic, oc_g, kd, kh, kw] This has no modulo/division in the inner loop! And no invalid kernel positions. Every input-kernel pair produces exactly one output position. The issue is that multiple (id, kd) pairs can map to the same od. For stride=2, an output position od receives from multiple inputs: od=0: id=0, kd=1 or id=1, kd=-1(invalid). Actually with pd=1, od=0 receives from: id*2 + kd - 1 = 0 id=0, kd=1 id=1, kd=-1(invalid) So only 1 input for this od. But for larger strides and paddings, there might be multiple. Wait, for a fixed od, there is at most one valid (id, kd) pair for each id (or for each kd). Because kd = od + pd - id*sd, and kd must be in [0, k). For a given od, as id varies, kd changes, and at most a few ids give valid kd. Actually, in terms of write conflicts: each thread processing (id, ih, iw, ic, kd, kh, kw) writes to a unique output position (od, oh, ow) for a fixed oc. But different threads with the same ic and different (id, kd) but same resulting od would write to the same position. Is this possible? od = id1*sd + kd1 - pd = id2*sd + kd2 - pd. id1*sd + kd1 = id2*sd + kd2. With sd=2: 2*id1 + kd1 = 2*id2 + kd2. If id1 = id2, then kd1 = kd2 (same pair). If id1 = id2 + 1, then 2*id2 + 2 + kd1 = 2*id2 + kd2, so kd2 = kd1 + 2. If kd1=0 and kd2=2, both valid for kD=3, then id1 = id2 + 1 gives the same od! Example: id2=0, kd2=2: od=0*2+2-1=1. id1=1, kd1=0: od=1*2+0-1=1. Yes! Both map to od=1. So input-stationary has write conflicts when multiple (id, kd) pairs give the same od. This means if multiple threads process different input positions that contribute to the same output position, we need atomics. But wait, if we process input positions sequentially, there's no conflict issue. We could have each thread handle one input position and all its kernel positions, accumulating to output. But then multiple threads write to the same output position. Actually, the natural way for input-stationary without atomics is to have each thread or block be responsible for computing all contributions to a contiguous output tile. That is, process all input positions that could affect a given output tile. This is essentially the same as output-stationary but iterated differently. Given the complexity, output-stationary is probably safer and easier to implement correctly. And for our sizes, the warp divergence might not be too bad. Let me reconsider the performance. Actually, the modulo condition (od + pd - kd) % sd == 0 with sd=2 is just a parity check: kd and (od + pd) must have the same parity. For our case: pd=1, so kd and od+1 must have same parity, i.e., kd and od must have opposite parity. So for a given od, valid kd are those with kd % 2 != od % 2 (and within bounds and giving valid id). Similarly for oh and kh: ph=2, sh=2, so kh and oh must have the same parity (since oh + 2 - kh even means kh and oh have same parity). For ow and kw: pw=3, sw=2, so kw and ow must have opposite parity. This is much cheaper than modulo! We can just check parity. For id >= 0: kd <= od + pd. If kd > od + pd, invalid. For id < D_in: (od + pd - kd) / 2 < D_in, i.e., od + pd - kd < 2*D_in. These bounds further restrict kd. For the warp divergence issue: for a given od, about half of kd values have the right parity. For a warp with 32 threads across 4 different od values (since blockDim.x=8, 32 threads = 4 od values if each od has 8 oc), each warp covers 4 consecutive od values. Since sd=2, consecutive od values alternate parity, so the valid kd pattern alternates too. This means that as we iterate over kd, a warp processes 4 different od values, and for each kd, roughly 2 of the 4 od values will find it valid (the ones with matching parity). This is 50% thread utilization within a warp per kd. But we only loop over 3 kd values. Can we restructure to avoid this? Alternative: process all valid kd values for each od. Here's a refined kernel design: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int kD, int kH, int kW, int sD, int sH, int sW, int pD, int pH, int pW, int groups) { int c_out = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int oc_group = c_out % (C_out / groups); int g = c_out / (C_out / groups); int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y * blockDim.y + threadIdx.y; if (spatial_idx >= D_out * H_out * W_out) return; int ow = spatial_idx % W_out; int tmp = spatial_idx / W_out; int oh = tmp % H_out; int od = tmp / H_out; float accum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in / groups; ++ic) { int in_c = g * (C_in / groups) + ic; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_val = od + pD - kd; if (d_val < 0 || d_val % sD != 0) continue; int id = d_val / sD; if (id >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int h_val = oh + pH - kh; if (h_val < 0 || h_val % sH != 0) continue; int ih = h_val / sH; if (ih >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int w_val = ow + pW - kw; if (w_val < 0 || w_val % sW != 0) continue; int iw = w_val / sW; if (iw >= W_in) continue; float in_val = input[((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in + id) * H_in * W_in + ih * W_in + iw]; float w_val = weight[((in_c * (C_out/groups) + oc_group) * kD + kd) * kH * kW + kh * kW + kw]; accum += in_val * w_val; } } } } output[((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + od) * H_out * W_out + oh * W_out + ow] = accum; } ``` But this has each thread handle one output channel and one spatial point. No. Standard conv formula in PyTorch: Given input size L_in, kernel k, stride s, padding p: L_out = floor((L_in + 2*p - k) / s) + 1 And for output position od, the input positions accessed are: id_start = od * s - p id = id_start + kd for kd in [0, k) So id = od * s + kd - p. For the transpose conv (adjoint), if output_grad at position od contributes to input_grad at id via kernel kd, then: input_grad[id] += output_grad[od] * weight[kd] where id = od * s + kd - p. For the transpose conv forward pass, we have "input" playing the role of output_grad and "output" playing the role of input_grad: output[id] = sum over od, kd where id = od * s + kd - p and valid od, kd: input[od] * weight[kd] Wait, but this maps from od to id, so the output is indexed by id and input by od. That means: ConvTranspose3d(x)[id] = sum_{od, kd: id = od*s + kd - p} x[od] * weight[kd] For a given output position id, the contributing inputs are: For each kd in [0, k): od = (id + p - kd) / s if this is integer and od in [0, L_in): valid So the formula is: od = (id + p - kd) / s with divisibility and bound checks. And the weight used is weight[kd]. In terms of channels, if Conv3d maps C_in → C_out with weight W[out, in/g, ...], then ConvTranspose3d maps C_out → C_in with weight W^T, i.e., weight[ic, oc_g, ...] = W[oc, ic_g, ...]. So: ConvTranspose3d(x)[ic, id] = sum over oc, kd where od = (id + p - kd)/s valid: x[oc, od] * W[oc, ic_g, kd] Wait, I keep mixing up input and output. Let me set variables clearly. In PyTorch: Conv3d: input (N, C_in, ...), weight (C_out, C_in/g, k...), output (N, C_out, ...) ConvTranspose3d: input (N, C_in, ...), weight (C_in, C_out/g, k...), output (N, C_out, ...) The mathematical relationship: ConvTranspose3d with weight W_T is the adjoint of Conv3d with weight W. If Conv3d(x) = y where y[oc] = sum_{ic, kd} x[ic, id(od, kd)] * W[oc, ic_g, kd] then the adjoint operation (which maps y to x) is: Adjoint(y)[ic, id] = sum_{oc, kd, od where id = od*s + kd - p} y[oc, od] * W[oc, ic_g, kd] For ConvTranspose3d forward: the "input" to ConvTranspose3d is like y above, and the "output" is like the adjoint result: ConvTranspose3d(input)[ic, id] = sum_{oc, kd, od where id = od*s + kd - p} input[oc, od] * W[oc, ic_g, kd] But wait, PyTorch ConvTranspose3d output has C_out channels, not C_in! So my channel mapping is backwards. ACTUALLY: if Conv3d maps C_in → C_out, then its adjoint maps C_out → C_in. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d also maps C_in → C_out, where C_in is the "input" channels and C_out is the "output" channels. So ConvTranspose3d[in→out] is NOT the adjoint of Conv3d[in→out]. Instead, ConvTranspose3d[in→out] with weight W_T is the adjoint of Conv3d[out→in] with weight W. That is: Conv3d: input of size C_out, weight (C_in, C_out/g, k...), output of size C_in ConvTranspose3d: input of size C_in, weight (C_in, C_out/g, k...), output of size C_out And: ConvTranspose3d(x)[oc, id] = sum_{ic, kd, od where id = od*s + kd - p} x[ic, od] * W[ic, oc_g, kd] Where x has C_in channels, and the weight W in ConvTranspose3d has shape (C_in, C_out/g, k...). With N=8, total 221,184 blocks. For H100, maximum concurrent blocks per SM is 32. With 132 SMs, max 4,224 concurrent blocks. 221K blocks is more than enough to saturate the GPU. Let me consider whether blockDim.y=4 or 8 is better. - blockDim.y=4: 128 threads/block. 221K blocks. Occupancy: 128 threads = 4 warps. On H100, max 2048 threads per SM, so 16 blocks per SM. But register/shared memory limits might apply. With ~32 registers per thread, 128*32=4096 registers per block. Max 64K per SM, so ~16 blocks per SM. That's good. - blockDim.y=8: 256 threads/block. 110K blocks. Occupancy: 256 threads = 8 warps. ~8 blocks per SM. Also good. More threads per block might have better memory coalescing for output writes if we process a 2D tile instead of just a 1D strip. Actually, blockDim.y=4 means each block handles 4 consecutive spatial positions (if we order them as (od, oh, ow) in row-major). Consecutive spatial positions in the linearized index correspond to consecutive W coordinates (since W is fastest-varying). So 4 consecutive W values. When writing output, for a fixed (oc, od, oh), we write to ow, ow+1, ow+2, ow+3. These are contiguous in memory. With blockDim.x=32, each warp handles one spatial position and all 32 channels. So warp 0 writes to (oc=0..31, od, oh, ow), warp 1 writes to (oc=0..31, od, oh, ow+1), etc. Perfect coalescing. I think 128 threads per block is a good starting point. Let me also consider whether I should parallelize over groups explicitly. Since C_out=32 and out_cpg=8, each group has 8 output channels. With blockDim.x=32, each block handles all 4 groups. This is fine. For memory accesses: as the block processes different (od, oh, ow) positions, the input positions accessed are different. But since the block processes a small tile of W positions, and W is the fastest-varying dimension in memory, consecutive threads might have good spatial locality for input. Actually, for a fixed od, oh but varying ow by 1: - iw = (ow + pw - kw) / sw For consecutive ow values, iw either stays the same or changes by 1 (depending on parity and kw). So consecutive output positions in W typically access nearby or the same input positions. This is good for caching. Let me write the kernel. In CUDA, coalesced reads require threads to access consecutive 4-byte words (or within the same 128-byte cache line, ideally). 420 bytes / 4 = 105 floats apart. For ow odd: valid kw must be even (kw = 0, 2, 4, ...). So for a warp with 16 even ow and 16 odd ow, if we iterate over all kw, half the threads will be active per kw. But if we instead iterate only over valid kw per thread, different threads iterate different kw sets. To get coalesced input reads, we'd want all active threads to read consecutive memory. With mixed ow parities and a single kw, the valid iw values for the 16 active threads might not be consecutive. Actually, for kw=1 (valid for even ow): ow=0: iw=1 ow=2: iw=2 ow=4: iw=3 ... ow=30: iw=16 These are 16 threads accessing iw=1..16. Perfectly coalesced! For kw=0 (valid for odd ow): ow=1: iw=2 ow=3: iw=3 ow=5: iw=4 ... ow=31: iw=17 Also perfectly coalesced! So if we process kw=0 and kw=1 in separate iterations, each iteration has 16 active threads with perfectly coalesced reads. The other 16 threads are inactive (diverged). After both iterations, all threads have done their work. Similarly for other kw values. This is quite efficient! The warp divergence is 50%, but the active threads have perfect coalescing. For kh and kd, the same pattern applies but since all threads in the warp have the same od and oh, the kh and kd loops are warp-uniform. So overall, the kernel has: - Warp-uniform branches for kd and kh. - 50% warp-active for kw, but perfect coalescing within the active threads. This is very efficient. I could further optimize by having each thread precompute its valid kw set and process them in a specific order. But the compiler might do a decent job already. One more thing: can I optimize the kw loop by explicitly handling parity? For each thread: if (ow % 2 == 0) { // valid kw are odd: 1, 3, 5 } else { // valid kw are even: 0, 2, 4, 6 } This avoids the modulo operation for each kw. But the modulo result is the same across iterations, so a smart compiler would hoist it anyway. I'll write it cleanly. Actually, the kw loop only has 7 iterations. For our specific case, sD=sH=sW=2, so these are simple right shifts. But in the general case, they're divisions. Since the compiler knows `sD` is a runtime constant (passed as argument), it won't optimize it to a shift unless the kernel is specialized. But since it's a runtime parameter, the division is a real integer division. However, we only do division after confirming `% sD == 0`, so it's exact division. The compiler might still generate a division instruction. To optimize, we could use `__builtin_assume` or similar, but that's compiler-specific. For our problem, the division is not the bottleneck anyway. Let me also think about whether I should process spatial positions in a different order for better cache locality. Currently, blocks are ordered as (W_tile, od*oh, n*c_pair). For od*oh, I use linearization where oh varies fastest. So consecutive blockIdx.y values have the same od but consecutive oh. This means consecutive blocks access input channels at the same D slices but consecutive H slices. Since H is the second-fastest varying dimension in memory, this should have good locality. Actually, wait. In the kernel, each block handles one (od, oh) for all W in a tile. The input access for a given (ic, kd, kh) reads a full H_in * W_in slice at id. But since id = (od + pd - kd) / sD, and od varies across blocks, consecutive blocks with different od access different id slices. So the D dimension locality depends on od. For our grid: blockIdx.y varies oh fastest (for fixed od). So consecutive blocks have the same od, same id for a given kd, but different oh. For a given oh, the input H accessed is ih = (oh + ph - kh) / sh. Consecutive oh means consecutive ih (when valid). So consecutive blocks access consecutive rows in the H_in * W_in plane. This is very cache-friendly. For W access within a block: threads access consecutive iw values for valid kw. This is also cache-friendly. Great. OK let me finalize the code. Let me write it now. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline import math # CUDA and C++ code cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int kD, int kH, int kW, int sD, int sH, int sW, int pD, int pH, int pW, int groups) { int n = blockIdx.z / (C_out / blockDim.y); int oc_base = (blockIdx.z % (C_out / blockDim.y)) * blockDim.y; int c_out = oc_base + threadIdx.y; int spatial_idx = blockIdx.y; int oh = spatial_idx % H_out; int od = spatial_idx / H_out; int ow = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (n >= N || c_out >= C_out || ow >= W_out) return; int out_cpg = C_out / groups; int in_cpg = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / out_cpg; int oc_g = c_out % out_cpg; float accum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_cpg; ++ic) { int in_c = g * in_cpg + ic; int w_base = (in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD * kH * kW; int in_offset_base = ((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in + 0) * H_in * W_in; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_val = od + pD - kd; if (d_val < 0 || (d_val % sD) != 0) continue; int id = d_val / sD; if (id >= D_in) continue; int in_offset_d = in_offset_base + id * H_in * W_in; int w_kd = w_base + kd * kH * kW; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int h_val = oh + pH - kh; if (h_val < 0 || (h_val % sH) != 0) continue; int ih = h_val / sH; if (ih >= H_in) continue; int in_offset_h = in_offset_d + ih * W_in; int w_kh = w_kd + kh * kW; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int w_val_coord = ow + pW - kw; if (w_val_coord < 0 || (w_val_coord % sW) != 0) continue; int iw = w_val_coord / sW; if (iw >= W_in) continue; float in_val = input[in_offset_h + iw]; float w_val = weight[w_kh + kw]; accum += in_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) { accum += bias[c_out]; } int out_offset = ((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + od) * H_out * W_out + oh * W_out + ow; output[out_offset] = accum; } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t sD, int64_t sH, int64_t sW, int64_t pD, int64_t pH, int64_t pW, int64_t out_pad_d, int64_t out_pad_h, int64_t out_pad_w, int64_t groups) { TORCH_CHECK(input.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "weight must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(input.dim() == 5, "input must be 5D"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 5, "weight must be 5D"); int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int D_in = input.size(2); int H_in = input.size(3); int W_in = input.size(4); int C_out_weight = weight.size(0); int out_cpg_weight = weight.size(1); int kD = weight.size(2); int kH = weight.size(3); int kW = weight.size(4); TORCH_CHECK(C_out_weight == C_in, "ConvTranspose3d weight shape mismatch"); int C_out = out_cpg_weight * groups; int D_out = (D_in - 1) * sD - 2 * pD + kD + out_pad_d; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * sH - 2 * pH + kH + out_pad_h; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * sW - 2 * pW + kW + out_pad_w; TORCH_CHECK(D_out > 0 && H_out > 0 && W_out > 0, "Invalid output size"); auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); dim3 block(32, 2, 1); dim3 grid((W_out + block.x - 1) / block.x, D_out * H_out, N * C_out / block.y); conv_transpose3d_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, kD, kH, kW, sD, sH, sW, pD, pH, pW, groups ); Let me verify from first principles using the convolution transpose formula. If regular convolution computes y = C * x where C is the convolution matrix, then transpose convolution computes C^T * x. Let me implement a correct version: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel(...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_elements = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total_elements) return; // Unflatten idx to (n, c_out, od, oh, ow) int ow = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int oh = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int od = tmp % D_out; tmp /= D_out; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int n = tmp / C_out; int g = c_out / out_cpg; int oc_g = c_out % out_cpg; float accum = 0.0f; for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < in_cpg; ++ic_local) { int in_c = g * in_cpg + ic_local; int in_base = ((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in) * H_in * W_in; int w_base = ((in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD) * kH * kW; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_mapped = od + pD - kd; if (d_mapped < 0 || (d_mapped % sD) != 0) continue; int id = d_mapped / sD; if (id >= D_in) continue; int in_d_offset = in_base + id * H_in * W_in; int w_kd_offset = w_base + kd * kH * kW; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int h_mapped = oh + pH - kh; if (h_mapped < 0 || (h_mapped % sH) != 0) continue; int ih = h_mapped / sH; if (ih >= H_in) continue; int in_h_offset = in_d_offset + ih * W_in; int w_kh_offset = w_kd_offset + kh * kW; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int w_mapped = ow + pW - kw; if (w_mapped < 0 || (w_mapped % sW) != 0) continue; int iw = w_mapped / sW; if (iw >= W_in) continue; float in_val = input[in_h_offset + iw]; float w_val = weight[w_kh_offset + kw]; accum += in_val * w_val; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) accum += bias[c_out]; int out_offset = ((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + od) * H_out * W_out + oh * W_out + ow; output[out_offset] = accum; } ``` This looks correct. Grid: - x = ceil(W_out / 32) - y = D_out * H_out - z = N * groups For each (od, oh), we process all output channels and W positions. Now, what are the memory access patterns? - Input: For a fixed (od, oh, ow) and variable output channels, the input accessed depends only on the kernel, not on the output channel. So threads with same threadIdx.x but different threadIdx.y for a fixed block (same od, oh) access the SAME input positions. - Weight: threads with same threadIdx.y access the same weight values for different (kd, kh, kw). This is great for data reuse! All 256 threads will access the same set of input spatial positions (but 8 different weight vectors, one per output channel). Actually, I need to think more carefully. For a fixed (n, g, od, oh, ow), the input channels accessed are all in group g, and for each input channel ic_local, the spatial input position is (id, ih, iw) determined by the output position and kernel position. For a fixed od and oh but varying ow and varying output channels: - Varying ow changes iw but not id or ih (for a given kd, kh). - Varying output channel doesn't change the input positions at all. So if a block handles fixed od, oh, and varying ow and varying c_out: - All threads in the block access the same range of (id, ih) positions for each (kd, kh). - Only iw varies with threadIdx.x and kw. This means the input data accessed by the block for a given (kd, kh) is essentially a strip along W. The total input size per (kd, kh) for 32 consecutive ow positions is about 32/sW + some offset = about 16 input positions, which is tiny. So the optimal strategy is: 1. Load all weights for the group into shared memory (27KB). 2. For each (kd, kh), compute the range of (id, ih) that this block needs, then load a strip of input W positions into shared memory. 3. Compute. Even better: since we have 256 threads, we can use them to cooperatively load data. Wait, but for a fixed (od, oh), and varying ow in [tile_start, tile_start+31], with sW=2, the corresponding iw values for a fixed kw are: iw = (ow + pW - kw) / sW For valid ow, iw ranges over at most ceil(32/sW) + 1 = 17 positions. And for different kw (0..6), the iw shifts by at most 6 positions. So for a fixed (kd, kh), the total unique iw values needed by the block for all kw is at most 17 + 6 = 23 positions. For all (kd, kh) combinations, id and ih also vary, but id and ih are the same for all ow in the block. For a fixed block (od, oh): - For each (kd, kh), id and ih are fixed (or absent if invalid). - We need a 2D-ish input region: for each valid (kd, kh), we need a strip of W_in positions. Actually, this is getting complex to implement. For correctness and timing, let me write a simpler but still coalesced kernel. Simpler kernel design: - Block = (32, 8, 1) = 256 threads (threadIdx.x for W, threadIdx.y for c_out in group). - Grid = (ceil(W_out/32), D_out*H_out, N*groups). - Each block processes: batch n, group g, spatial (od, oh), W tile. - All threads in a block access the same n and same group. - First, load all weights for the group into shared memory (27KB). - Then, process all input channels. Processing steps: 1. Load weights into shmem. Each thread loads some weights. Total 27KB / 256 threads ≈ 27*4/256 ≈ 0.4 floats per thread. Actually 6720 floats / 256 ≈ 26 floats per thread. Easy. 2. For each input channel `ic_local` in 0..in_cpg-1: - For each valid (kd, kh) combination for the block's (od, oh): - Compute id and ih. If invalid, skip. - Determine the range of iw values needed for this W tile and all kw values. - Load these input values into shared memory. - Compute partial products. Wait, computing id and ih for each (kd, kh) is independent of W. And for each (kd, kh, kw), the iw values vary but in a predictable way. Actually, for a direct implementation, maybe I don't need shared memory for input. Let me just focus on the weight shared memory and keep input in global memory. Since we have 8 output channels, each thread reads input once and multiplies by 8 different weights. So input is read 8 times less than in the naive approach (because 8 threads share the same input reads). With the block layout (W=32, c_out=8), all 8 threads with the same threadIdx.x (same ow) but different threadIdx.y read the same input positions. In a warp, 32 threads with different threadIdx.x read different ow -> different iw. Warp divergence: threads in a warp with consecutive threadIdx.x will read memory with a stride, which might be okay for L2 cache. For the naive kernel where each thread handles only one output element, this kernel where threads share input for the same ow but different c_out will have much better input reuse. Actually, I can do even simpler: in the loop over kernel positions, each thread checks valid ow positions. The key insight is that for a fixed od, oh and varying ow with step 32, threads in a warp read consecutive ow. For L2 cache, consecutive ow with stride 32 are somewhat localized. Let me just write the block layout kernel with weight in shared memory and iterate naturally: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int kD, int kH, int kW, int sD, int sH, int sW, int pD, int pH, int pW, int out_pad_d, int out_pad_h, int out_pad_w, int groups) { extern __shared__ float sh_mem[]; // First part of shared memory: weights for the group int n = blockIdx.z / groups; int g = blockIdx.z % groups; int od = blockIdx.y / H_out; int oh = blockIdx.y % H_out; int ow = blockIdx.x * 32 + threadIdx.x; int c_out = g * (C_out / groups) + threadIdx.y; if (n >= N || c_out >= C_out || ow >= W_out) return; int in_cpg = C_in / groups; int out_cpg = C_out / groups; int oc_g = threadIdx.y; // c_out within group // Load weights into shared memory // Weight layout: (in_channels, out_cpg, kD, kH, kW) // Offset for group g: g * in_cpg * out_cpg * kD * kH * kW // But weight is indexed as weight[in_c * out_cpg + oc_g][kd][kh][kw] int total_group_weights = in_cpg * out_cpg * kD * kH * kW; float* sh_weight = sh_mem; // Each thread loads some weights for (int i = threadIdx.y * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; i < total_group_weights; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { sh_weight[i] = weight[(g * in_cpg * out_cpg * kD * kH * kW) + i]; } __syncthreads(); float accum = 0.0f; int in_base_n_c = ((n * C_in + g * in_cpg) * D_in) * H_in * W_in; for (int ic_local = 0; ic_local < in_cpg; ++ic_local) { int in_base = in_base_n_c + ic_local * D_in * H_in * W_in; int w_ic_base = ic_local * out_cpg * kD * kH * kW; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_mapped = od + pD - kd; if (d_mapped < 0 || d_mapped % sD != 0) continue; int id = d_mapped / sD; if (id >= D_in) continue; int in_d_offset = in_base + id * H_in * W_in; int w_kd_base = w_ic_base + oc_g * kD * kH * kW + kd * kH * kW; // Wait ``` Wait, my weight indexing in shared memory is getting mixed up. For a specific (in_c, oc_g), the offset in the full weight array is: ((in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD + kd) * kH * kW + kh * kW + kw For shared memory, I loaded weights for the group starting from the global offset corresponding to group g. For ConvTranspose2d, the standard relationship to direct convolution is: - Direct conv: stride=s, padding=p, kernel=k, input=i, output=o. - Transposed conv: stride=s, padding=p, kernel=k, input=o, output=i. If standard conv computes y = Cx, then transposed conv computes x = C^T y. But conceptually, in deep learning, transpose conv is usually defined as: output[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_c_in sum_kd,kh,kw input[n, c_in, ?, ?, ?] * weight[c_in, c_out_g, kd, kh, kw] where the spatial relationship is: d = d_in * stride_d + kd - padding_d Wait, but that's the EXACT SAME formula as I had earlier for my kernel. Is that right? Actually yes! In "transposed convolution", each input element is expanded into a kernel-sized patch in the output. So an input element at (d_in, h_in, w_in) contributes to output positions: (d_in * stride_d + kd - padding_d, ...) for all valid (kd, kh, kw). This means for a fixed output position (d, h, w), the contributing input positions are those where: d = d_in * stride_d + kd - padding_d => d_in = (d + padding_d - kd) / stride_d Which is the SAME formula I derived. Actually, in standard conv, `input[d_in * stride + kd - padding]` uses the same `kd` for all spatial positions. The kernel is NOT flipped along the spatial dimensions. For transpose conv, each input element is multiplied by the kernel and scattered to output. The `kd` in the weight is the exact same `kd` - there's no flip involved because we use cross-correlation, not convolution. So my formula should be correct! I'll be very careful with integer types and the exact structure. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline import math cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int C_out, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int kD, int kH, int kW, int sD, int sH, int sW, int pD, int pH, int pW, int groups) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int n = tmp / C_out; int out_cpg = C_out / groups; int in_cpg = C_in / groups; int g = c_out / out_cpg; int oc_g = c_out % out_cpg; float accum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_cpg; ++ic) { int in_c = g * in_cpg + ic; int in_base = ((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in) * H_in * W_in; int w_base = ((in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD) * kH * kW; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_mapped = od + pD - kd; kh < kH; ++kh) { int h_mapped = oh + pH - kh; if (h_mapped < 0 || h_mapped % sH != 0) continue; int ih = h_mapped / sH; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in) continue; int in_h_offset = in_d_offset + ih * W_in; int w_kh_offset = w_kd_offset + kh * kW; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int w_mapped = ow + pW - kw; if (w_mapped < 0 || w_mapped % sW != 0) continue; int iw = w_mapped / sW; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; accum += input[in_h_offset + iw] * weight[w_kh_offset + kw]; } } } } if (bias != nullptr) { accum += bias[c_out]; } int out_offset = ((n * C_out + c_out) * D_out + od) * H_out * W_out + oh * W_out + ow; output[out_offset] = accum; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t sD, int64_t sH, int64_t sW, int64_t pD, int64_t pH, int64_t pW, int64_t out_pad_d, int64_t out_pad_h, int64_t out_pad_w, int64_t groups) { TORCH_CHECK(input.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "weight must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(input.dim() == 5, "input must be 5D"); TORCH_CHECK(weight.dim() == 5, "weight must be 5D"); int N = input.size(0); int C_in = input.size(1); int D_in = input.size(2); int H_in = input.size(3); int W_in = input.size(4); int kD = weight.size(2); int kH = weight.size(3); int kW = weight.size(4); TORCH_CHECK(weight.size(0) == C_in, "ConvTranspose3d weight shape mismatch"); int C_out = weight.size(1) * groups; int D_out = (D_in - 1) * sD - 2 * pD + kD + out_pad_d; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * sH - 2 * pH + kH + out_pad_h; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * sW - 2 * pW + kW + out_pad_w; auto output = torch::empty({N, C_out, D_out, H_out, W_out}, input.options()); int total_elements = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total_elements + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.has_value() ? bias.value().data_ptr<float>() : nullptr, output.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, C_out, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, kD, kH, kW, sD, sH, sW, pD, pH, pW, groups ); return output; } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int64_t sD, int64_t sH, int64_t sW, int64_t pD, int64_t pH, int64_t pW, int64_t out_pad_d, int64_t out_pad_h, int64_t out_pad_w, int64_t groups); ''' conv_transpose3d_cuda = load_inline( name="conv_transpose3d_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["conv_transpose3d_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=(1,1,1), padding=(0,0,0), output_padding=(0,0,0), groups=1, bias=False): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.in_channels = in_channels self.out_channels = out_channels self.kernel_size = kernel_size self.stride = stride self.padding = padding self.output_padding = output_padding self.groups = groups self.weight = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(in_channels, out_channels // groups, *kernel_size)) if bias: self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(out_channels)) else: self.register_parameter('bias', None) nn.init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5)) if self.bias is not None: fan_in, _ = nn.init._calculate_fan_in_and_fan_out(self.weight) bound = 1 / math.sqrt(fan_in) nn.init.uniform_(self.bias, -bound, bound) def forward(self, x): if not x.is_cuda: x = x.cuda() if not self.weight.is_cuda: self.weight.data = self.weight.data.cuda() if self.bias is not None: self.bias.data = self.bias.data.cuda() return conv_transpose3d_cuda.conv_transpose3d_forward( x, self.weight, self.bias, self.stride[0], self.stride[1], self.stride[2], self.padding[0], self.padding[1], self.padding[2], self.output_padding[0], self.output_padding[1], self.output_padding[2], self.groups ) ``` Wait, I should check if we need to call `cudaGetLastError()` in the C++ wrapper. For the given problem: - in_channels=32, out_channels=32, groups=4 - stride=2, padding=(1,2,3), kernel=(3,5,7) - D_in=12, H_in=24, W_in=48 Each output element needs to sum over in_cpg=8 input channels and kernel positions. But since each thread reads its own input/weight with minimal cache reuse, it will likely be memory-bound. Weight memory: 32*8*3*5*7 = 26,880 floats = 107 KB. Input memory: 8*32*12*24*48 = 3.54M floats = 14.2 MB. Output memory: 8*32*24*48*96 = 28.3M floats = 113.2 MB. For good cache utilization: - The weights (107 KB) can fit in L2 cache. - The input (14.2 MB) can partially fit in L2 cache but might spill. If each output element reads ~24*8 = 192 input values (with some overlapping but not much due to strided access patterns), total input reads = 28.3M * 192 ≈ 5.4B reads. But with cache, many of these input reads might be shared. Wait, input positions are heavily reused! Each input element contributes to a LOT of output elements. Specifically, each input element at (id, ih, iw) contributes to output positions: For each (kd, kh, kw) where 0<=kd<3, 0<=kh<5, 0<=kw<7: od = id*2 + kd - 1, oh = ih*2 + kh - 2, ow = iw*2 + kw - 3. For a single input element, it contributes to 3*5*7 = 105 output positions. But each of those 105 positions is in the output of a DIFFERENT output channel? No! It contributes to ALL output channels in the same group. Since out_cpg=8, each input element contributes to 8*105 = 840 output values. So if a warp/thread block processes multiple output channels for the same spatial position, they can share the same input values. This is the key to optimization. My previous block layout suggestion (32 threads for W, 8 threads for c_out) is actually really good! All threads in the block with the same threadIdx.x but different threadIdx.y access the SAME input values for the same (od, oh, ow) but multiply by different weights for different output channels. Wait, for a fixed (od, oh, ow), the input values needed depend only on the output position, not on the output channel. So all 8 threads computing different output channels for the same (od, oh, ow) need the exact same input values but different weights. However, in my flat kernel, each thread handles a random output element. Threads in the same block might or might not share input values, depending on the flat index mapping. Let me redesign the kernel with the block layout (32, 8, 1): - threadIdx.y: output channel within group (0..7) - threadIdx.x: W position within tile (0..31) - blockIdx.y: (od, oh) spatial position - blockIdx.x: W tile - blockIdx.z: batch * group This way, all threads in a block for the same (blockIdx.x, blockIdx.y, blockIdx.z) process the same n, g, od, oh but different ow and oc_g. They all share the same input channels! Specifically: - For fixed od, oh, varying ow and oc_g, the needed input channels are all in group g. - For a fixed (kd, kh) and varying ow, the input id and ih are fixed. Only iw varies. - But more importantly, ALL threads in the block access input from the SAME input channels (in group g). If we preload the input for the required (id, ih) combinations into shared memory, all threads can read from shared memory. Actually, we don't even need shared memory for great speedup. Since all threads access the same input values (just different weights), and the weights are also accessed in a structured way, the L1 cache might capture a lot of this. But let me compute: in a block of 256 threads, if 8 threads share the same ow but use different oc_g, and there are 32 distinct ow values: - Total distinct (ow, oc_g) = 32 * 8 = 256 output elements. - For each output element, ~24 valid input positions (on average) across all input channels. - But with 8 input channels and ~2*3*4=24 kernel positions, total distinct input values needed = 8 * ~24, but wait, for different ow, the iw varies. Actually, for fixed od, oh, and varying ow in a tile of 32: - For each (kd, kh), id and ih are fixed. - For each kw, the iw values correspond to iw = (ow + pW - kw) / sW for valid ow. - As ow ranges over 32 values and kw ranges over 7 values, the set of iw values is roughly a contiguous block of size ~32/sW + 7 ≈ 16 + 7 = 23. - For 8 input channels, total distinct input values = 8 * 2 * 3 * 23 ≈ 1,104 floats = 4.4 KB. This is easily cacheable in L1! (L1 cache per SM on H100 is 128 KB per SM, but per block it depends on occupancy). For weights: - Each oc_g needs 8 * 3 * 5 * 7 = 840 floats. - 8 output channels need 8 * 840 = 6,720 floats = 26.9 KB. This is also cacheable. So with the (32, 8) block layout, the total working set fits in L1 cache! This means the kernel could be very fast. But implementing this requires: 1. Handling the 2D block layout correctly. 2. Computing the output elements for each thread. Let me implement this properly. Kernel parameters: - Block: (32, 8, 1) - Grid: - x: (W_out + 31) / 32 - y: D_out * H_out - z: N * groups Inside the kernel: ```cuda int n = blockIdx.z / groups; int g = blockIdx.z % groups; int od = blockIdx.y / H_out; int oh = blockIdx.y % H_out; int ow = blockIdx.x * 32 + threadIdx.x; int oc_g = threadIdx.y; int c_out = g * out_cpg + oc_g; if (n >= N || ow >= W_out) return; float accum = 0.0f; for (int ic = 0; ic < in_cpg; ++ic) { int in_c = g * in_cpg + ic; int in_base = ((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in) * H_in * W_in; for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) { int d_mapped = od + pD - kd; if (d_mapped < 0 || d_mapped % sD != 0) continue; int id = d_mapped / sD; if (id >= D_in) continue; int in_d = in_base + id * H_in * W_in; int w_base = ((in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD + kd) * kH * kW; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) { int h_mapped = oh + pH - kh; if (h_mapped < 0 || h_mapped % sH != 0) continue; int ih = h_mapped / sH; if (ih >= H_in) continue; int in_h = in_d + ih * W_in; int w_kh = w_base + kh * kW; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; ++kw) { int w_mapped = ow + pW - kw; if (w_mapped < 0 || w_mapped % sW != 0) continue; int iw = w_mapped / sW; if (iw >= W_in) continue; accum += input[in_h + iw] * weight[w_kh + kw]; } } } } ``` This is essentially the same code but launched with a 2D block layout. The memory access pattern is much better because threads in a warp (which have consecutive threadIdx.x, same threadIdx.y) process consecutive ow values. And threads in the same block for different threadIdx.y but same threadIdx.x access the same input but different weights. For memory access: - `input[in_h + iw]`: Threads in a warp with consecutive threadIdx.x but same threadIdx.y access different iw values. Since iw = (ow + pW - kw) / sW, consecutive ow might map to non-consecutive iw, but the pattern is deterministic and somewhat localized. - `weight[w_kh + kw]`: All threads with the same threadIdx.y access the same weight addresses. So a warp of 32 threads would have 4 warps with different threadIdx.y values (assuming 8 threads in y, that's 8 groups of identical accesses). Since warp size is 32, and threadIdx.x ranges from 0..31, all 32 threads in a warp have the SAME threadIdx.y. Thus, ALL threads in a warp access the SAME weights at the same time. This is perfectly uniform! Great for memory bandwidth. For input accesses: all 32 threads in a warp access potentially DIFFERENT input values (because ow differs). But since ow are consecutive, iw might be spread out with stride = 1/sW on average, which means semi-random access for small sW. For sW=2, iw values for consecutive ow that are both valid for the same kw are spread out by about 0.5 (alternating valid/invalid). The valid iw values themselves increase by 1 for every increase of 2 in ow. So in a warp, the accessed iw values are roughly half of the 32 ow values, and among those, the iw values form a strided pattern. This means memory access pattern is: approximately 16 different iw values, each accessed by 2 threads. These iw values are spread over a range of about 16 positions in W_in. If W_in=48, and the range is contiguous-ish, this might fit in a cache line or a few. Actually, iw values are roughly integer, and if the start offset is aligned, the 16 accesses might span 16 floats = 64 bytes = 1 cache line. But depending on the exact values, they might span multiple cache lines. Still, 16 cache lines per warp is manageable. So this block layout should give quite good performance! Let me implement it this way. Oh, one important note: across different `oc_g` values, the input accesses are identical. So when the warp scheduler executes warps for different `oc_g`, the L1 cache already has the input data loaded. This is great! Actually, threadIdx.y ranges from 0 to 7. So there are 8 warps in each block for each threadIdx.x value? No. Block = (32, 8, 1). Total threads = 256. - Warp size = 32. - Each warp consists of threads with consecutive threadIdx.x and the SAME threadIdx.y. - So there are 8 warps per block. - Warp 0: threadIdx.y = 0, threadIdx.x = 0..31 - Warp 1: threadIdx.y = 1, threadIdx.x = 0..31 - etc. When warp 0 executes and loads input values, those input values populate L1 cache. Then when warp 1 executes for the same block, it accesses the SAME input values but different weights. Since the input is already in L1 cache, warp 1 gets L1 cache hits. This is perfect! So my block layout provides automatic L1 cache sharing for input, and uniform weight access per warp. This should be quite fast. For the W dimension: iw = (ow + pW - kw) / sW For this to be a valid integer, (ow + pW - kw) % sW == 0. Since sW=2, we can check parity: (ow + pW - kw) % 2 == 0 => (ow % 2) == ((kw - pW) % 2) Since pW=3, and kw ranges from 0 to 6: kw - 3 mod 2: kw=0: 1 kw=1: 0 kw=2: 1 kw=3: 0 kw=4: 1 kw=5: 0 kw=6: 1 Since ow % 2 alternates between 0 and 1 for consecutive threads in a warp, exactly half of the kw values will be valid for each thread. Specifically: - If threadIdx.x is even (ow even), valid kw are those where kw is odd (kw=1,3,5). - If threadIdx.x is odd (ow odd), valid kw are those where kw is even (kw=0,2,4,6). So for each thread, we only need to iterate over 4 or 3 kw values instead of all 7, AND we avoid the % check! Similarly for H and D: sH=2, pH=2: h_mapped = oh + 2 - kh For h_mapped % 2 == 0: (oh + 2 - kh) % 2 == 0 => oh % 2 == kh % 2. So if oh is even, valid kh are even (0,2,4). If oh is odd, valid kh are odd (1,3). sD=2, pD=1: d_mapped = od + 1 - kd For d_mapped % 2 == 0: (od + 1 - kd) % 2 == 0 => od % 2 != kd % 2. So if od is even, valid kd are odd (1). Let me structure the code cleanly: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import math from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward(...) { ... } ''' cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int has_bias, int64_t sD, int64_t sH, int64_t sW, int64_t pD, int64_t pH, int64_t pW, int64_t out_pad_d, int64_t out_pad_h, int64_t out_pad_w, int64_t groups); In subsequent forward calls, `self.conv_transpose3d` is already on CUDA. But it might break references held outside the model (unlikely). More importantly, PyTorch's `.cuda()` returns a new module instance but also modifies the original? Actually, `.cuda()` creates a new module on the target device and returns it. So `self.conv_transpose3d` now points to the CUDA version. The original CPU version becomes garbage-collectible unless there are other references. This is fine. But a simpler approach is: ```python if not x.is_cuda: x = x.cuda() w = self.conv_transpose3d.weight if not w.is_cuda: w = w.cuda() self.conv_transpose3d.weight.data = w if self.conv_transpose3d.bias is not None: self.conv_transpose3d.bias.data = self.conv_transpose3d.bias.data.cuda() ``` Wait, modifying `.data` of a Parameter can break autograd if gradients are needed. But we don't care about autograd for this task. Actually, the easiest thing: just call `.cuda()` on the tensors when passing them: ```python x = x.cuda() w = self.conv_transpose3d.weight.cuda() b = self.conv_transpose3d.bias.cuda() if self.conv_transpose3d.bias is not None else None ``` This creates temporary CUDA tensors without modifying the model. If the evaluator only runs forward once, this is fine. If it runs multiple times, each call will copy the weights from CPU to GPU again. So I should probably cache them on GPU. But looking at KernelBench, it typically runs forward once for correctness and then once for timing (or warmups + timing). So caching matters. Let me do: ```python def forward(self, x): if not x.is_cuda: x = x.cuda() if not self.conv_transpose3d.weight.is_cuda: self.conv_transpose3d = self.conv_transpose3d.cuda() w = self.conv_transpose3d.weight b = self.conv_transpose3d.bias ... ``` This moves the whole module to GPU once, and subsequent calls reuse GPU parameters. Actually, `self.conv_transpose3d = self.conv_transpose3d.cuda()` replaces the module with its CUDA copy. This should be fine. Or more simply, I could just move the model in the evaluator's test script. The test script creates `model_new` and then calls `.cuda()` on it before `forward`. But I can't control the test script. Looking at the provided test code, there's no `.cuda()` call. So the model is on CPU. If the evaluator uses the same test code, then both `Model` and `ModelNew` will be on CPU. But the evaluator might wrap them in `.cuda()` before testing. To be safe, I'll assume GPU execution and just move everything to GPU in `forward` if needed. This is a common pattern. One final thing: `self.conv_transpose3d.stride` returns a tuple. So `self.conv_transpose3d.stride[0]` is the stride in depth. Alright, I'll write the complete code now. Actually, in `load_inline`, the Python side passes Python ints, which get converted to `int64_t` for C++ functions. So my C++ parameter types should be `int64_t`. Let me finalize the CUDA kernel code. I'll use `int` inside the kernel for the loop variables because CUDA prefers 32-bit ints for indexing (faster than 64-bit on some architectures). And I'll compute `out_cpg = C_out / groups` as `int`. Since C_out and groups are passed as `int`, integer division is fine. One more thought: since W_out = 96 which is divisible by 32, grid.x = ceil(96/32) = 3. But for safety, I should compute using `(W_out + 31) / 32` in the C++ wrapper. OK here's my final kernel code. turn 4llm latency: 56.5s
reasoning
The problem is that the CUDA kernel is in the `cuda_sources` but the C++ code in `cpp_sources` can't call it since CUDA syntax (`<<<>>>`) is not valid C++. I need to put everything in the `cuda_sources` only, or have `cpp_sources` as a header for pybind11 and the full CUDA code in `cuda_sources` containing the kernel AND the wrapper that does the `<<<>>>` launch. Actually `load_inline` auto-generates `PYBIND11_MODULE`, but it needs `functions=[...]` to name the functions. The C++ wrapping function can be in the `cuda_sources` (compiled by nvcc), while `cpp_sources` can be cpp-only declarations, but we cannot have `<<<>>>` in `cpp_sources`. We also cannot have the kernel function declaration in `cpp_sources` without the actual kernel implementation being accessible. Since nvcc compiles `cuda_sources`, we should put the whole kernel + launcher in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` either empty or with the declarations for pybind11. Actually, since `load_inline` auto-generates `PYBIND11_MODULE` from `functions=[...]`, the functions should be exposed via something that pybind can bind to. But the auto-generated binding uses `m.def(...)` to bind the functions. So as long as the functions are available at the C++ level, they will get bound. Let me restructure: put the kernel and the wrapper in `cuda_sources`, and leave `cpp_sources` empty or minimal.

blockIdx.z / C_out;\n int c_out = blockIdx.z % C_out;\n int od = blockIdx.y / H_out;\n int oh = blockIdx.y % H_out;\n int ow = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n\n if (n >= N || ow >= W_out) return;\n\n int out_cpg = C_out / groups;\n int in_cpg = C_in / groups;\n int g = c_out / out_cpg;\n int oc_g = c_out % out_cpg;\n\n float accum = 0.0f;\n\n for (int ic = 0; ic < in_cpg; ++ic) {\n int in_c = g * in_cpg + ic;\n int in_base = ((n * C_in + in_c) * D_in) * H_in * W_in;\n int w_base = ((in_c * out_cpg + oc_g) * kD) * kH * kW;\n\n for (int kd = 0; kd < kD; ++kd) {\n int d_mapped = od + pD - kd;\n if (d_mapped < 0 || d_mapped % sD != 0) continue;\n int id = d_mapped / sD;\n if (id < 0 || id >= D_in) continue;\n\n int in_d_offset = in_base + id * H_in * W_in;\n int w_kd_offset = w_base + kd * kH * kW;\n\n for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; ++kh) {\n int h_mapped = oh + pH - kh;\n if (h_mapped < 0 || h_mapped % sH != 0) continue;\n int ih = h_mapped / sH;\n if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in)
